The Scholarly Journey of Alaa al-Din Ali al-Qujhasari
Biographical notice of education, travels, and works

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the educational path and scholarly contributions of Alaa al-Din Ali al-Qujhasari as recorded in al-Shaqa'iq al-Nu'maniyya.

Core Idea
A scholar's journey from local study to distant masters shapes his legacy.

This lesson introduces a brief biographical notice from al-Shaqa'iq al-Nu'maniyya about the scholar Alaa al-Din Ali al-Qujhasari. Although only a few details survive, his travels to study under renowned scholars like al-Taftazani and al-Sayyid al-Sharif, and his own gloss on an important work, reveal his dedication and skill. By examining this passage, we gain insight into the traditional educational path of 8th century AH scholars.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then follow the English translation. Notice the structure: it begins with his teachers, then travels, then works.
2
Identify key figures and places
Note the names of the scholars he studied under (al-Taftazani, al-Sayyid al-Sharif) and the regions he traveled to (Bilad al-Ajam, Bilad al-Rum). What does this tell about scholarly networks?
3
Reflect on the significance of his gloss
The commentary says his gloss (hashiya) demonstrates skill in Arabic sciences. Think about why glosses were important in Islamic scholarship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٦ - علاء الدين علي القو جحصاري:
يقول عنه صاحب الشقائق النعمانية : قرأ على علماء عصره ثم ارتحل إلى بلاد العجم وقرأ على العلامة التفتازاني، والسيد الشريف، ثم ارتحل إلى بلاد الروم . وفي تلك البلاد البعيدة فوض إليه تدريس بعض المواد في إحدى المدارس وصنف بعض الكتب منها :
١ - حاشية على شرح المفتاح للعلامة التفتازاني .
ويتابع صاحب الشقائق حديثه بقوله : ويفهم من تلك الحاشية أن له مهارة تامة في العلوم العربية، لأنها حاشية مقبولة أورد فيها تحقيقات كثيرة .
ولم يورد صاحب الشقائق شيئاً لا من قريب ولا من بعيد عن حياته وتاريخ مولده ووفاته. وإن كان تاريخ الوفاة يكاد يكون على وجه التقريب في النصف الأول من القرن الثامن الهجري رحمه الله (۲)
6 - Ala al-Din Ali al-Qujhasari: The author of al-Shaqa'iq al-Nu'maniyya says about him: He studied under the scholars of his time, then traveled to the lands of the non-Arabs and studied under the eminent scholar al-Taftazani and al-Sayyid al-Sharif, then traveled to the lands of the Romans. In those distant lands, the teaching of some subjects in one of the schools was entrusted to him, and he authored some books including: (1) A gloss on Sharh al-Miftah by al-Allama al-Taftazani. And the author of al-Shaqa'iq continues his speech saying: And it is understood from that gloss that he had complete skill in the Arabic sciences, because it is an accepted gloss in which he presented many investigations. And the author of al-Shaqa'iq did not mention anything, neither near nor far, about his life and the date of his birth and death. Although the date of death is almost approximately in the first half of the eighth century AH. May God have mercy on him (2).
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is a biographical notice of Ala al-Din Ali al-Qujhasari, as recorded by the author of al-Shaqa'iq al-Nu'maniyya. It describes his education: he first studied with scholars of his time, then traveled to 'the lands of the non-Arabs' (likely Persia) to study under the famous scholars al-Taftazani and al-Sayyid al-Sharif. He then traveled to the 'lands of the Romans' (Anatolia), where he was appointed to teach some subjects at a school. Among his authored works is a gloss (hashiya) on Sharh al-Miftah by al-Taftazani, which demonstrates his proficiency in the Arabic sciences. The biographer notes that he did not provide details of his birth and death, but estimates his death date roughly in the first half of the 8th century AH (14th century CE).

حاشية على شرح المفتاح

Muhammad 'Ata' Allah: Lineage, Education, and Early Career
Opening biography with lineage, birth, education, and early appointments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the disputed lineage of Muhammad 'Ata' Allah, his educational background as a Hanafi-turned-Shafi'i student of al-Taftazani, and his early career honors from Nawruz and the Mamluk sultan.

Core Idea
A scholar's lineage may be uncertain, but his achievements are witnessed by history.

Muhammad 'Ata' Allah al-Harawi's biography begins with a puzzle: he claimed descent from the great Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, but historians doubt it. Yet his own scholarly journey—from Herat to Damascus to Cairo—is well-documented. This lesson traces his lineage, education under al-Taftazani, and the honors he received from rulers.

Guided Path
1
Read about the lineage dispute
Notice the two proposed genealogies and the skepticism of the author's teacher. The passage emphasizes uncertainty.
The teacher's remark shows caution in accepting unverified claims.
2
Trace his education and madhhab change
He began as a Hanafi, then became Shafi'i, and studied under al-Taftazani. Consider what this says about scholarly flexibility.
3
Examine his early career honors
Nawruz appointed him to a teaching post in Damascus, and later the Mamluk sultan received him with great ceremony in Cairo.
The sultan seated him to his right—a mark of high honor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۷ - محمد عطاء الله بن بن محمد: اختلف في نسبه اختلافاً بيناً فقيل بعد ذلك هو أحمد بن محمود بن الإمام فخر الدين محمد بن عمر .
وقيل: محمود بن أحمد بن فضل الله بن محمد الشمس أبو عبد الله بن أبي الجود وأبي البركات الرازي الأصل الهروي.
هكذا كان يزعم أنه من بني الفخر الرازي، قال شيخنا: ولم نقف على صحة ذلك، ولا بلغنا من كلام أحد من المؤرخين أنه كان للإمام ولد ذكر . فالله أعلم .
ولد بهراة سنة سبع وستين وسبعمائة، واشتغل في بلاده حنفياً ثم تحول شافعياً، وأخذ عن التفتازاني .
اجتمع به نوروز صاحب مملكة الشام وولاه تدريس الصلاحية بعد الشهاب ابن الهاشم.
وقدم القاهرة في صفر سنة ثماني عشرة فخرج الطبغا العثماني لتلقيه وصعد به إلى القلعة وبالغ السلطان في إكرامه وأجلسه عن يمينه، ثم أنزله بدار أعدت له .
قال الجمال الطيماني : إنه يحل الكتب المشكلة ويتخلص فيها، وصنف شرح مسلم وغيره وبنى بالقدس مدرسة ولم تتم (۱) .
وقال العيني : كان عالماً فاضلاً متفنناً له تصانيف كشرح مشارق الأنوار وشرح صحيح مسلم المسمى فضل المنعم وشرح الجامع الكبير من أوائله ولم يكمله. وكان قد أدرك الكبار مثل التفتازاني والسيد، وصارت له حرمة في البلاد واسعة وخصوصاً سمرقند وهراة ، حتى كان الحاكم يعظمه ويحترمه ويميزه على غيره بحيث يدخل عنده في حريمه ويستشيره، وربما كان يرسله في مهماته، ولذا قيل : إنه وزيره، وليس كذلك
وقال المقريزي : إنه ولي القضاء وكتابة السر، وكان يقرر في المذهبين ويعرف العربية وعلمي البيان والبديع ويذاكر الأدب والتاريخ، ويستحضر كثيراً
(۱) راجع الضوء اللامع ج ١ ص ١٤١ - ١٤٥ وهدية العارفين ج ١ ص ١٨٤ .
7 - Muhammad 'Ata' Allah son of [ ] son of Muhammad: There was clear disagreement about his lineage. It was said that he is Ahmad ibn Mahmud ibn al-Imam Fakhr al-Din Muhammad ibn 'Umar. And it was said: Mahmud ibn Ahmad ibn Fadl Allah ibn Muhammad al-Shams Abu 'Abd Allah ibn Abi al-Jud and Abi al-Barakat, of Razi origin, from Herat. Thus he claimed to be from the family of Fakhr al-Razi. Our shaykh said: We have not verified the correctness of that, nor has it reached us from the speech of any historian that the Imam had a male child. God knows best. He was born in Herat in the year 767 H, and studied in his country as a Hanafi, then became Shafi'i, and studied under al-Taftazani. Nawruz, the ruler of the kingdom of Sham, met him and appointed him to the teaching position at al-Salahiyya after al-Shihab ibn al-Hashim. He came to Cairo in Safar of the year 818 H. The 'Uthmani tabgha came out to receive him and ascended with him to the citadel. The sultan was excessive in honoring him and seated him to his right, then lodged him in a house prepared for him. Al-Jamal al-Taymani said: He solves difficult books and extracts from them. He authored a commentary on Sahih Muslim and other works, and built a school in Jerusalem that was not completed (1). Al-'Ayni said: He was a virtuous, excellent, and versatile scholar. He had works such as Sharh Mashariq al-Anwar, Sharh Sahih Muslim called Fadl al-Mun'im, and Sharh al-Jami' al-Kabir from its beginning, which he did not complete. He had met the greats like al-Taftazani and al-Sayyid, and acquired wide respect in the lands, especially Samarkand and Herat, to the extent that the ruler honored him, respected him, and distinguished him over others, so that he would enter his private quarters and consult him, and sometimes send him on his important missions. Hence it was said that he was his vizier, but it is not so. Al-Maqrizi said: He held the position of judge and secretary of the chancellery, and he would expound on the two madhhabs, knew Arabic and the sciences of al-bayan and al-badi', and would discuss literature and history, and would recall many things. (1) Refer to al-Daw' al-Lami' vol. 1 pp. 141-145 and Hadiyyat al-'Arifin vol. 1 p. 184.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is a detailed biography of Muhammad 'Ata' Allah al-Harawi. It begins by noting the disagreement over his lineage, with two proposed genealogies, both tracing back to Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, though the author's teacher doubts that al-Razi had a son. He was born in Herat in 767 H, initially studied as a Hanafi then became Shafi'i, and studied under al-Taftazani. Nawruz, a ruler, appointed him to a teaching post in Damascus. In 818 H he went to Cairo, where he was received with great honor by the sultan. The biographer al-Jamal al-Taymani praises his ability to solve difficult books and mentions his commentary on Sahih Muslim and an unfinished school in Jerusalem. Al-'Ayni describes him as a versatile scholar with works including commentaries on Mashariq al-Anwar, Sahih Muslim, and al-Jami' al-Kabir. He had met major scholars and enjoyed high status in Samarkand and Herat, being consulted by rulers. Al-Maqrizi notes he held judicial and secretarial offices, was knowledgeable in both Hanafi and Shafi'i schools, Arabic, rhetoric, and history. The passage gives a comprehensive view of his scholarly career.

Key Terms
محمد عطاء الله
فخر الدين الرازي
التفتازاني
نوروز
الطبغا العثماني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may assume that Muhammad 'Ata' Allah's claimed descent from Fakhr al-Din al-Razi is verified.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that this claim is not confirmed; the author's teacher notes no historian records that al-Razi had a male child.

Checkpoint

A) 767 H
B) 818 H
C) 700 H
D) 854 H

Key Takeaway
Muhammad 'Ata' Allah's early life shows a scholar whose lineage is uncertain but whose education and honors from rulers are historically recorded.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your lineage or background may be uncertain, just like Muhammad 'Ata' Allah's. In what ways do you rely on your family name, ethnicity, or social status to feel secure? How would your self-worth change if you truly believed that Allah alone determines your honor—by your taqwa, not your ancestry? Commit to one concrete action this week that affirms your identity as a servant of Allah, independent of human recognition.
?
Muhammad 'Ata' Allah was honored by rulers because of his knowledge, not his lineage. But what honors do you seek most—from people or from Allah? Are you willing to spend your life in pursuit of knowledge and piety even if it goes unnoticed by the world? Write a short prayer asking Allah to make you seek His pleasure above all else, and recite it daily for the next week.

Scholarly Works and Reputation of Muhammad 'Ata' Allah
Continuation of biography: scholarly works and testimonies from historians

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly works and reputation of Muhammad 'Ata' Allah as described by the historians al-'Ayni and al-Maqrizi.

Core Idea
His versatility and wide respect are attested by his works and the testimonies of later scholars.

The second part of his biography shifts to his intellectual legacy. Al-Jamal al-Taymani highlights his ability to solve difficult books. Al-'Ayni lists his major works, including commentaries on hadith collections, and notes his high status in Samarkand and Herat. Al-Maqrizi adds that he served as judge and secretary, and was well-versed in multiple disciplines.

Al-'Ayni said: He was a virtuous, excellent, and versatile scholar. He had works such as Sharh Mashariq al-Anwar, Sharh Sahih Muslim called Fadl al-Mun'im, and Sharh al-Jami' al-Kabir from its beginning, which he did not complete. He had met the greats like al-Taftazani and al-Sayyid, and acquired wide respect in the lands, especially Samarkand and Herat, to the extent that the ruler honored him, respected him, and distinguished him over others, so that he would enter his private quarters and consult him, and sometimes send him on his important missions. Hence it was said that he was his vizier, but it is not so. Al-Maqrizi said: He held the position of judge and secretary of the chancellery, and he would expound on the two madhhabs, knew Arabic and the sciences of al-bayan and al-badi', and would discuss literature and history, and would recall many things. (1) Refer to al-Daw' al-Lami' vol. 1 pp. 141-145 and Hadiyyat al-'Arifin vol. 1 p. 184.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is a detailed biography of Muhammad 'Ata' Allah al-Harawi. It begins by noting the disagreement over his lineage, with two proposed genealogies, both tracing back to Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, though the author's teacher doubts that al-Razi had a son. He was born in Herat in 767 H, initially studied as a Hanafi then became Shafi'i, and studied under al-Taftazani. Nawruz, a ruler, appointed him to a teaching post in Damascus. In 818 H he went to Cairo, where he was received with great honor by the sultan. The biographer al-Jamal al-Taymani praises his ability to solve difficult books and mentions his commentary on Sahih Muslim and an unfinished school in Jerusalem. Al-'Ayni describes him as a versatile scholar with works including commentaries on Mashariq al-Anwar, Sahih Muslim, and al-Jami' al-Kabir. He had met major scholars and enjoyed high status in Samarkand and Herat, being consulted by rulers. Al-Maqrizi notes he held judicial and secretarial offices, was knowledgeable in both Hanafi and Shafi'i schools, Arabic, rhetoric, and history. The passage gives a comprehensive view of his scholarly career.

المقريزي

Key Takeaway

The Life and Legacy of Muhammad ibn Ahmad al-Harawi
Biographical entry of a Shafi'i scholar from Herat with details on his life, works, and controversies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the life, scholarly journey, and contributions of the Shafi'i scholar Muhammad ibn Ahmad al-Harawi, including his education, career, and the controversies surrounding him.

Core Idea
A scholar's life reveals the interconnected world of Islamic learning across regions and schools.

Biographical entries in traditional Islamic works are not just historical records; they offer lessons in scholarly dedication, the pursuit of knowledge, and the challenges scholars face. This passage introduces us to Muhammad ibn Ahmad al-Harawi, a Shafi'i scholar from Herat who studied under greats like al-Taftazani, served under rulers, and left significant works. As we read, we will see how his life reflects the broader intellectual currents of his time.

Guided Path
1
Read the biography carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get an overview of al-Harawi's life events and the key details presented.
Pay attention to the sequence of events from birth to death.
2
Note his scholarly transformation
Observe his initial Hanafi affiliation and later conversion to the Shafi'i school, and his study under al-Taftazani.
This shift reflects the fluidity of madhhab affiliation in his time.
3
Identify key achievements and roles
Look at his teaching position in Cairo, his works, and the honors he received from rulers.
The list of works at the end is important for his scholarly legacy.
4
Understand the controversies
Note the accusations against him and the author's defense, as well as the doubts about his lineage.
These controversies show the challenges scholars faced.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيل: محمود بن أحمد بن فضل الله بن محمد الشمس أبو عبد الله بن أبي الجود وأبي البركات الرازي الأصل الهروي. هكذا كان يزعم أنه من بني الفخر الرازي، قال شيخنا: ولم نقف على صحة ذلك، ولا بلغنا من كلام أحد من المؤرخين أنه كان للإمام ولد ذكر . فالله أعلم . ولد بهراة سنة سبع وستين وسبعمائة، واشتغل في بلاده حنفياً ثم تحول شافعياً، وأخذ عن التفتازاني . اجتمع به نوروز صاحب مملكة الشام وولاه تدريس الصلاحية بعد الشهاب ابن الهاشم. وقدم القاهرة في صفر سنة ثماني عشرة فخرج الطبغا العثماني لتلقيه وصعد به إلى القلعة وبالغ السلطان في إكرامه وأجلسه عن يمينه، ثم أنزله بدار أعدت له . قال الجمال الطيماني : إنه يحل الكتب المشكلة ويتخلص فيها، وصنف شرح مسلم وغيره وبنى بالقدس مدرسة ولم تتم . وقال العيني : كان عالماً فاضلاً متفنناً له تصانيف كشرح مشارق الأنوار وشرح صحيح مسلم المسمى فضل المنعم وشرح الجامع الكبير من أوائله ولم يكمله. وكان قد أدرك الكبار مثل التفتازاني والسيد، وصارت له حرمة في البلاد واسعة وخصوصاً سمرقند وهراة ، حتى كان الحاكم يعظمه ويحترمه ويميزه على غيره بحيث يدخل عنده في حريمه ويستشيره، وربما كان يرسله في مهماته، ولذا قيل : إنه وزيره، وليس كذلك . وقال المقريزي : إنه ولي القضاء وكتابة السر، وكان يقرر في المذهبين ويعرف العربية وعلمي البيان والبديع ويذاكر الأدب والتاريخ، ويستحضر كثيراً من الأحاديث والناس فيه بين غال ومقصر . وقال غيره : كان شيخاً ضخماً طوالاً أبيض اللحية مليح الشكل إلا أن في لسانه مسكة ، إماماً بارعاً في فنون من العلوم له تصانيف تدل على غزير علمه واتساع نظره وتبحره في العلوم، وكان يركب بعد ولايته البغلة بهيئة الأعاجم بفرجيه وعذبه مرخية على يساره فأقام مدة ثم لبس زي قضاة مصر وساق الأبيات التي وجدها المؤيد وأولها . يا أيها الملك المؤيد دعوة من مخلص في حبه لك يفصح ثم إن غالب الفقهاء تعصبوا عليه وبالغوا في التشنج ، ورموه بعظائم الظن براءته عن أكثرها ، رحمه الله رحمة واسعة . من مصنفاته : ١ - تعريف الأحكام في فروع الشافعية . ٢ - التمحيص في شرح التلخيص للجامع الكبير من فروع الحنفية . ٣ - التنوير في تلخيص الجامع الكبير للشيباني في الفروع . ٤ - شرح مصابيح السنة للبغوي . ٥ - المنعم بشرح الجامع الصحيح المسلم
And it was said: Mahmud (or Muhammad) ibn Ahmad ibn Fadl Allah ibn Muhammad al-Shams Abu Abdullah ibn Abi al-Jud and Abi al-Barakat al-Razi al-Harawi. Thus he claimed to be from the descendants of Fakhr al-Razi. Our shaykh said: We have not verified the truth of that, nor has it reached us from any historian that the Imam had a male child. God knows best. He was born in Herat in the year 767 AH, and studied in his country as a Hanafi, then converted to Shafi'i, and studied under al-Taftazani. Nawruz, the ruler of the land of Syria, met with him and appointed him to teach at the Salahiyya school after Shihab ibn al-Hashim. He came to Cairo in Safar 818 AH, so the Ottoman Talbagha came out to receive him and took him to the citadel; the sultan honored him greatly and seated him on his right, then lodged him in a house prepared for him. Al-Jamal al-Taymani said: He solves difficult books and extracts their meanings. He authored a commentary on Muslim and other works, and built a school in Jerusalem but it was not completed. Al-Ayni said: He was a learned, virtuous, and versatile scholar; he has works such as a commentary on Mashariq al-Anwar, a commentary on Sahih Muslim called Fadl al-Munim, and a commentary on al-Jami al-Kabir from its beginning but did not complete it. He met great scholars like al-Taftazani and al-Sayyid, and gained wide respect in the lands, especially Samarkand and Herat, to the point that the ruler honored him, respected him, and distinguished him over others, so that he would enter his private quarters and consult him, and sometimes he would send him on important missions, so it was said that he was his vizier, but it was not so. Al-Maqrizi said: He held the positions of judge and secretary; he would teach in both schools (Hanafi and Shafi'i), knew Arabic, the sciences of bayan and badi', discussed literature and history, and recalled many hadiths; people were either extreme or neglectful regarding him. Someone else said: He was a huge, tall shaykh with a white beard and handsome appearance, except that he had a speech impediment, an outstanding imam in various sciences; his works indicate his abundant knowledge, breadth of view, and deep erudition in the sciences. After his appointment, he used to ride a mule in the manner of the Persians, with a farajiyya and his belt hanging down to his left; he remained like that for a while, then he adopted the dress of the judges of Egypt and recited the verses that al-Mu'ayyad had found, beginning: O al-Mu'ayyad the king, a call from one sincere in his love for you, he declares. Then most of the jurists became prejudiced against him and went to extremes in hostility, and accused him of great things, but we think he is innocent of most of them. May God have abundant mercy on him. Among his works: 1. Ta'rif al-Ahkam in Shafi'i furu'; 2. al-Tamhis fi Sharh al-Talkhis li al-Jami' al-Kabir from Hanafi furu'; 3. al-Tanwir fi Talkhis al-Jami' al-Kabir li al-Shaybani in furu'; 4. Sharh Masabih al-Sunna li al-Baghawi; 5. al-Mun'im bi Sharh al-Jami' al-Sahih al-Muslim.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is a biographical entry for a scholar named Mahmud (or Muhammad) ibn Ahmad al-Harawi, a Shafi'i scholar from Herat who lived around the 8th-9th centuries AH. The biography begins by noting his claimed descent from Fakhr al-Razi but expresses doubt about its veracity. It records his birth in Herat in 767 AH, his initial Hanafi studies and his later conversion to the Shafi'i school, and his studies under the famous scholar al-Taftazani. The text then recounts his meeting with Nawruz, the ruler of Syria, and his appointment to teach at the Salahiyya school in Cairo after the Shihab ibn al-Hashim's tenure. It describes his arrival in Cairo in 818 AH, where he was received by the Ottoman dignitary al-Talbagha and honored by the sultan. Various contemporary scholars are quoted praising his abilities: al-Jamal al-Taymani notes his skill in solving difficult texts; al-Ayni describes his scholarly output including commentaries on Mashariq al-Anwar and Sahih Muslim, and his association with great scholars like al-Taftazani and al-Sayyid. Al-Maqrizi mentions his judicial and secretarial roles and his competence in both Hanafi and Shafi'i law, Arabic, rhetoric, and history. Another source describes his physical appearance and his initial adoption of Persian attire before adopting the dress of Egyptian judges. Finally, it notes that many jurists became prejudiced against him and accused him of serious offenses, but the author expresses belief in his innocence. The entry concludes with a list of his five main works.

The Nisba and Education of al-Karimi
Biographical entry detailing name, nisba, origins, and early education.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the origins, early education, and scholarly lineage of Muhammad ibn Fadl Allah al-Karimi.

Core Idea
A scholar's nisba can reflect both lineage and geography.

This lesson introduces the scholar al-Karimi, whose full name and multiple nisbas tell a story of his origins and scholarly connections. The passage from the biographical source explains the meaning of his name, his birth in Khwarazm, his upbringing in Bukhara, and his studies under notable teachers, including al-Taftazani. By understanding this biography, you gain insight into the educational networks of 8th-century Central Asia.

Guided Path
1
Read the full Arabic passage
Begin by reading the Arabic text carefully. Notice the structure: it starts with his full name, explains the nisba, then gives his geographical origins and early education.
Pay attention to the names of teachers: al-Mawla Abd al-Rahman and al-Taftazani.
2
Analyze the meaning of his nisbas
The passage offers two possible reasons for the nisba 'al-Karimi'. Reflect on how a scholar's name can indicate either a teacher or a father's honorific. Also note his other nisba: al-Khatibi.
The nisba 'al-Karimi' is explained in detail.
3
Map his geographical journey
Trace his life from Khwarazm (birth) to Bukhara (Qur'an and grammar studies) to Samarkand (residence). Consider the significance of these cities in Islamic scholarship.

4

الشمس الكريمي: هو محمد بن فضل الله بن المجد أحمد الشمس الكريمي بفتح أوله أو كسر (ثانيه نسبة لبعض مشايخ خوارزم وقيل بل لأبيه كريم الدين الخورزامي المولد البخاري المنشأ السمرقندي المسكن، الحنفي ويعرف في بلاده بالخطيبي، وبين المصريين بالكريمي. ولد في حدود سنة ثلاث وسبعين وسبعمائة بخوارزم، ثم انتقل به أبوه إلى بخارى فقرأ بها القرآن وأخذ النحو عن المولى عبد الرحمن، وكان يحضر عند التفتازاني ويأخذ منه
Al-Shams al-Karimi: He is Muhammad ibn Fadl Allah ibn al-Majd Ahmad al-Shams al-Karimi, with the first letter [of Karimi] fath or kasr, attributed to some shaykhs of Khwarazm, or it is said to his father Karim al-Din al-Khwarazmi by birth, al-Bukhari by upbringing, al-Samarqandi by residence, the Hanafi, and known in his country as al-Khatibi, and among the Egyptians as al-Karimi. He was born around the year 773 AH in Khwarazm, then his father moved him to Bukhara, where he studied the Qur'an and took grammar from al-Mawla Abd al-Rahman, and he used to attend al-Taftazani's lessons and learn from him.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage provides a brief biographical entry for the scholar known as al-Shams al-Karimi. It gives his full name as Muhammad ibn Fadl Allah ibn al-Majd Ahmad al-Shams al-Karimi, and explains the etymology of the nisba 'al-Karimi' — it may be related either to certain shaykhs of Khwarazm or to his father's name Karim al-Din. It notes his birth in Khwarazm around 773 AH, his upbringing in Bukhara, residence in Samarkand, and his Hanafi school affiliation. It mentions his popular names: al-Khatibi in his homeland and al-Karimi among Egyptians. The biography then briefly records his early education: he learned the Qur'an in Bukhara, studied grammar with al-Mawla Abd al-Rahman, and attended the lessons of the famous scholar al-Taftazani.

Biography of a Scholar: Appearance, Works, and Controversies
Biographical description with list of works.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical biographical entries describe a scholar's appearance, intellectual achievements, and the controversies they faced.

Core Idea
Biographical accounts reveal the human dimension of Islamic scholarship and the challenges scholars endured.

This passage introduces us to a remarkable scholar through the eyes of a biographer. It describes not only his physical appearance and intellectual output, but also the opposition he encountered from rivals. As you read, notice how the author balances praise with critique, providing a nuanced portrait.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation

2

The passage begins with a reference to people's extremes regarding a hadith.

Examine the list of works
The passage ends with a numbered list of five works. Study each title and infer the subjects they cover (fiqh, hadith, etc.).
4
Reflect on the biographer's tone

من الأحاديث والناس فيه بين غال ومقصر .

وقال غيره : كان شيخاً ضخماً طوالاً أبيض اللحية مليح الشكل إلا أن في لسانه مسكة ، إماماً بارعاً في فنون من العلوم له تصانيف تدل على غزير علمه واتساع نظره وتبحره في العلوم، وكان يركب بعد ولايته البغلة بهيئة الأعاجم بفرجيه وعذبه مرخية على يساره فأقام مدة ثم لبس زي قضاة مصر وساق الأبيات التي وجدها المؤيد وأولها .

From the hadiths, and people regarding it are between extreme and falling short. • And others said: He was a huge, tall old man with a white beard, handsome in appearance, except that he had a stammer in his tongue, an outstanding imam in the arts of the sciences; he had works indicating his abundant knowledge, breadth of view, and deep research in the sciences. He used to ride after his appointment a mule in the attire of the Persians with a farajiyya and a hanging lock on his left side. He stayed for a while, then wore the dress of the judges of Egypt and recited the verses that al-Mu'ayyad found, the first of which is: 'O king al-Mu'ayyad, a plea from a sincere one in your love he declares.' The Life and Teachers of al-Shams al-Karimi
Biography of al-Shams al-Karimi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the life and scholarly journey of al-Shams al-Karimi, including his teachers, travels, and final years.

Core Idea
Al-Shams al-Karimi's life reflects the classical Islamic scholarly path of travel and study under masters.

This passage presents the biography of Muhammad ibn Fadl Allah al-Shams al-Karimi, a Hanafi scholar known for his travels and studies with prominent figures like al-Taftazani and al-Sayyid al-Jurjani. By reading this biography, we glimpse the traditional educational system and the interconnected world of Islamic scholarship in the 8th-9th centuries AH. Let's explore his life step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then read the English translation. Notice the flow of information: name, birth, early education, teachers, travels, death, and character.
The passage includes a footnote (2) after al-Taftazani and (1) at the end.
2
Identify his name and origin
Note his full name, the explanation of 'al-Karimi', and his geographical affiliations: Khwarazm, Bukhara, Samarqand.
He is also known as al-Khatibi in his country and al-Karimi in Egypt.
3
List his teachers and studies
Find the names of his teachers: al-Mawla Abd al-Rahman, al-Taftazani, al-Nur al-Khwarazmi, al-Sayyid al-Jurjani. Note the subjects: Quran, grammar, al-Ma'ani, al-Badi', logic, usul al-din.
He studied Sharh al-Mawaqif and Sharh al-Matali' under al-Jurjani.
4
Trace his travels and death
Follow his journey: from Khwarazm to Bukhara, to Samarqand, to Cairo, to Damascus, and finally to Edirne. Note the dates and events like Hajj and invitation by Ibn Uthman.
He died in Edirne in early 861 AH.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشمس الكريمي: هو محمد بن فضل الله بن المجد أحمد الشمس الكريمي بفتح أوله أو كسر ثانيه نسبة لبعض مشايخ خوارزم وقيل بل لأبيه كريم الدين الخوارزمي المولد البخاري المنشأ السمرقندي المسكن، الحنفي ويعرف في بلاده بالخطيبي، وبين المصريين بالكريمي.
ولد في حدود سنة ثلاث وسبعين وسبعمائة بخوارزم، ثم انتقل به أبوه إلى بخارى فقرأ بها القرآن وأخذ النحو عن المولى عبد الرحمن، وكان يحضر عند التفتازاني ويأخذ منه (۲)
ثم انتقل إلى سمرقند فأخذ المعاني والبديع عن النور الخوارزمي ثم لازم السيد الجرجاني . حتى أخذهما مع شرح المواقف في أصول الدين، وشرح المطالع في المنطق وحواشيه .
وقدم للقاهرة للحج في جمادى الآخر سنة اثنين وخمسين فلازم الأقراء وانتفع به جماعة في كتب سعد الدين في المعاني والبيان .
وكذا دخل دمشق وأقرأ بها وممن قرأ عليه المنطق المشرف بن عبد، وكان نازلاً عنده وطلبه ابن عثمان ملك الروم عقب وفاة بعض علمائهم ليقيم عندهم فسافر ومات بأدرنة من بلاد الروم في أوائل سنة إحدى وستين وثمانمائة، وكان إماماً علامة صالحاً متواضعاً جم العلم كثير الحفظ، وكان في لسانه عقلة رحمه الله تعالى (۱).
Al-Shams al-Karimi: He is Muhammad ibn Fadl Allah ibn al-Majd Ahmad al-Shams al-Karimi, with fatha on its first letter or kasra on its second, in relation to one of the shaykhs of Khwarazm, and it is said rather to his father Karim al-Din. He was Khwarazmian by birth, Bukharan by upbringing, Samarqandi by residence, Hanafi, and known in his country as al-Khatibi, and among the Egyptians as al-Karimi. He was born around the year 773 AH in Khwarazm, then his father moved him to Bukhara, where he read the Quran and took grammar from al-Mawla Abd al-Rahman, and he attended al-Taftazani and learned from him (2). Then he moved to Samarqand, where he took al-Ma'ani and al-Badi' from al-Nur al-Khwarazmi, then he closely accompanied al-Sayyid al-Jurjani until he took them along with Sharh al-Mawaqif in Usul al-Din, and Sharh al-Matali' in Logic and its glosses. He came to Cairo for Hajj in Jumada al-Akhira 852 AH, stayed enjoying bounty (?), and a group benefited from him in the books of Sa'd al-Din on al-Ma'ani and al-Bayan. Likewise he entered Damascus and taught there; among those who read Logic to him was al-Mushrif ibn Abd, and he was staying with him. Ibn Uthman, the king of Rum, requested him after the death of one of their scholars to settle with them, so he traveled and died in Edirne in the land of Rum in early 861 AH. He was an imam, a scholar, righteous, humble, vast in knowledge, with great memory, and he had a speech impediment. May Allah have mercy on him (1).
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage is a biography of al-Shams al-Karimi (Muhammad ibn Fadl Allah). It provides his full name and explains the ascription 'al-Karimi' either to a shaykh or his father. It describes his geographical affiliations: born in Khwarazm, raised in Bukhara, settled in Samarqand, Hanafi in school. He studied the Qur'an and grammar in Bukhara, then attended al-Taftazani. In Samarqand, he studied al-Ma'ani and al-Badi' with al-Nur al-Khwarazmi and then closely followed al-Sayyid al-Jurjani, studying major works like Sharh al-Mawaqif and Sharh al-Matali'. He performed Hajj in 852 AH and stayed in Cairo, where he taught. He also went to Damascus, then was invited by the Ottoman Sultan Mehmed II (Ibn Uthman) after the death of a scholar, and died in Edirne. He is praised for his knowledge and piety, and is noted to have had a speech impediment.

Key Takeaway
Al-Shams al-Karimi was a prolific traveler and student of major scholars, embodying the pursuit of knowledge across the Islamic world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have read about al-Shams al-Karimi's relentless pursuit of knowledge, traveling across cities and sitting at the feet of great masters. What one thing have you done this week that required similar sacrifice for your own intellectual or spiritual growth? If the answer is nothing, what specific step will you take today to change that?
?
The source mentions that al-Shams al-Karimi was 'vast in knowledge, with great memory, and he had a speech impediment.' Reflect: Do you use your physical limitations as excuses to avoid seeking knowledge, or do you, like him, let them become insignificant in the face of your determination? What will you do this week to overcome one obstacle to your learning?
?
This scholar traveled from Khwarazm to Edirne, teaching and learning wherever he went. Consider your own life: Have you become too comfortable in your current environment to seek truth and understanding? Where might Allah be calling you to 'travel'—physically, mentally, or spiritually—to grow in knowledge of Him? Write down one concrete action you will take this week to step out of your comfort zone.

The Scholarly Network of Yusuf al-Halaj
Biography of Yusuf al-Halaj with his teachers and students.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly lineage and contributions of Yusuf al-Halaj as recorded in the biographical tradition.

Core Idea
A short biography reveals the interconnected world of Islamic scholarship.

This lesson presents a brief biographical note on Yusuf al-Halaj, a Shafi'i scholar from Herat. The passage situates him within a network of teachers and students, highlighting his commentary on al-Hawi and the praise of his student. As you read, notice how such biographies serve to authenticate chains of knowledge transmission.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Focus on the names and relationships mentioned.
The passage is dense with names; pay attention to how they are connected.
2
Identify the key figures
List the individuals mentioned: Yusuf al-Halaj, his father, al-Shams Muhammad al-Madi, al-Taftazani, his son al-Shams, Ahmad ibn Musa al-Hajiri, and al-Taqi al-Hisni. Notice the chain: father → al-Shams → al-Taftazani; and Yusuf → his son and al-Hajiri → al-Taqi.
This chain shows the transmission of knowledge through generations.
3
Reflect on the praise
Read the description by al-Taqi al-Husni. What does it tell us about Yusuf's status? Phrases like 'journeys are made to him' indicate he was a sought-after scholar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يوسف الحلاج هو يوسف الجمال الحلاج الهروي الشافعي، والد أخذ الشمس محمد الماضي ممن عن التفتازاني وغيره، وتقدم في الفضائل، وشرح الحاوي شرحاً متوسطاً وانتفع به الفضلاء كولده الشمس، ومحد بن موسى الحاجري شيخ التقي الحصني .
ويصفه تلميذه التقي الحسني بقوله : ممن تشد له الرحال ويعول عليه في كشف المقال والحال زبدة الأفاضل الماهرين الماجد الهمام جمال الدنيا والدین (۲).
Yusuf al-Halaj is Yusuf al-Jamal al-Halaj al-Harawi al-Shafi'i, whose father took from al-Shams Muhammad al-Madi from those who [studied] under al-Taftazani and others, and he excelled in virtues. He wrote a medium commentary on al-Hawi, and the virtuous benefited from him, such as his son al-Shams, and Ahmad [or Muhammad] ibn Musa al-Hajiri, the shaykh of al-Taqi al-Hisni. And his student al-Taqi al-Husni describes him saying: '[He is] one to whom journeys are made and upon whom reliance is placed in unveiling discourse and states, the cream of the excellent, the skilled, the glorious, the energetic, Jamal al-Dunya wa al-Din.' (2).
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage is a short biography of Yusuf al-Halaj, a Shafi'i scholar from Herat (al-Harawi). It notes his father studied with al-Shams Muhammad al-Madi, who had connections to al-Taftazani. Yusuf wrote a commentary on al-Hawi (a legal work). Scholars benefited from him, including his son al-Shams and Ahmad ibn Musa al-Hajiri, who was the teacher of al-Taqi al-Hisni. The passage concludes with a glowing description by his student al-Taqi al-Husni, praising him as one to whom people travel for knowledge and who is relied upon, calling him 'the cream of the excellent' and 'Jamal al-Dunya wa al-Din'.

?

?

If your own teacher were described that way, would you feel confident that they deserve that trust? Conversely, if you were to become a teacher, would your students be able to say the same of you with honesty?

Jalal al-Din's Connection to al-Taftazani and the Ijaza
Historical note on Jalal al-Din and his ijaza from al-Taftazani.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical connection between Jalal al-Din Yusuf ibn Rukn al-Din Masih and his teacher Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani, as evidenced by an ijaza authorizing him to work with al-Taftazani's writings.

Core Idea
An ijaza reveals the deep trust between a master and his student.

This short passage introduces a key figure in Islamic intellectual history: Jalal al-Din Yusuf ibn Rukn al-Din Masih. It records that some historians list him as a student of the renowned scholar Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani, and presents an ijaza (license) as evidence. The ijaza authorizes Jalal al-Din to alter, read, and correct al-Taftazani's works, reflecting a profound scholarly relationship.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: a statement about historical consensus followed by the content of the ijaza.
The passage ends with a colon, indicating the ijaza text itself follows later.
2
Identify key elements
Identify the three key elements: the student (Jalal al-Din), the teacher (al-Taftazani), and the nature of the ijaza (authorization to alter, read, and correct works).
3
Reflect on the significance of the ijaza
Consider what this ijaza implies about the relationship between al-Taftazani and his student. Why would a scholar grant such broad authority?
Think about traditional Islamic education and the trust built between teacher and student.

The Academic Journey of a Student of al-Taftazani
Biography of a scholar's education and death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the traditional scholarly journey of a student of al-Taftazani, including the cities, teachers, and texts studied.

Core Idea
A scholar's journey across lands seeking knowledge from masters shaped Islamic learning.

This passage presents the biography of a scholar who studied under prominent teachers in Samarkand, Cairo, and Damascus, and later taught others. The account illustrates the traditional path of Islamic education, moving from one center of learning to another, and highlights the role of personal transmission of texts. Through this narrative, you will see how scholarship was both a personal and communal endeavor.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze. Then read the translation. Let the flow of the scholar's journey come through.
Notice the sequence of cities and teachers.
2
Trace the geographical journey
Identify the cities mentioned: Samarkand, Cairo, Damascus, and Edirne. Consider why each location was significant in the Islamic world at that time.
3
Identify teachers and texts
List the teachers: al-Nur al-Khwarizmi, al-Sayyid al-Jurjani, al-Aqza'i. Note the texts: Sharh al-Mawaqif, Sharh al-Matali', and the books of Sa'd al-Din on al-ma'ani and al-bayan.
These are advanced theological and rhetorical works.
4
Reflect on character and death
Read the description of his virtues: an imam, scholar, righteous, humble, vast knowledge. Note his speech impediment. Consider the circumstances of his death: summoned by the Ottoman sultan, died in Edirne.
The biography ends with a prayer for mercy, a common feature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم انتقل إلى سمرقند فأخذ المعاني والبديع عن النور الخوارزمي ثم لازم السيد الجرجاني . حتى أخذهما مع شرح المواقف في أصول الدين، وشرح المطالع في المنطق وحواشيه . وقدم للقاهرة للحج في جمادى الآخر سنة اثنين وخمسين فلازم الأقـزاء وانتفع به جماعة في كتب سعد الدين في المعاني والبيان . وكذا دخل دمشق وأقرأ بها وممن قرأ عليه المنطق المشرف بن عبد، وكان نازلاً ،عنده وطلبه ابن عثمان ملك الروم عقب وفاة بعض علمائهم ليقيم عندهم فسافر ومات بأدرنة من بلاد الروم في أوائل سنة إحدى وستين وثمانمائة، وكان إماماً علامة صالحاً متواضعاً جم العلم كثير الحفظ، وكان في لسانه عقلة الله تعالى (۱). رحمه
Then he moved to Samarkand and studied al-ma'ani and al-badi' from al-Nur al-Khwarizmi, then he closely followed al-Sayyid al-Jurjani until he mastered them along with Sharh al-Mawaqif in usul al-din and Sharh al-Matali' in logic and its glosses. And he came to Cairo for Hajj in Jumada al-Akhira of the year 852, and he closely followed al-Aqza'i, and a group benefited from him in the books of Sa'd al-Din on al-ma'ani and al-bayan. Likewise, he entered Damascus and taught there, and among those who studied logic with him was al-Musharraf ibn Abd, who was staying with him. And Ibn Uthman, the king of Rum, requested him after the death of one of their scholars to stay with them, so he traveled and died in Edirne in the land of Rum in the beginning of the year 861. He was an imam, a scholar, righteous, humble, vast in knowledge, with much memorization, and he had a speech impediment from Allah (1). May Allah have mercy on him.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage is a biography of an unnamed scholar who was a student of Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani (indicated by 'books of Sa'd al-Din'). It follows his academic journey: first moving to Samarkand to study Arabic rhetoric (al-ma'ani and al-badi') under al-Nur al-Khwarizmi, then closely studying with al-Sayyid al-Jurjani until mastering theological and logical works. He later traveled to Cairo for Hajj in 852 AH and studied with al-Aqza'i, benefiting others through al-Taftazani's books. He also taught in Damascus, where al-Musharraf ibn Abd studied logic with him. The Ottoman sultan (Ibn Uthman) summoned him after a scholar's death, and he traveled there, dying in Edirne in 861 AH. The passage ends by praising his virtues, noting a speech impediment, and praying for mercy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What price are you willing to pay for the knowledge of your faith? Are there obstacles you use as excuses? How can you begin this very week to emulate his dedication and humility?
?

?
The scholar mastered multiple sciences: rhetoric, theology, logic. In our age of specialization, we often neglect integrated learning. Is there an area of Islamic knowledge you have ignored because it seemed difficult or unrelated to your current focus? What is one small step you can take this week to broaden your study?

Biography of Yusuf al-Hallaj
Biographical entry for Yusuf al-Hallaj with lineage, students, and works.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly lineage and contributions of Yusuf al-Hallaj as recorded in the biographical tradition.

Core Idea
Yusuf al-Hallaj was a Shafi'i scholar known for his commentary on al-Hawi and his influence on later scholars.

This lesson introduces a brief biographical entry from a classical source. It details the name, lineage, teachers, students, and a laudatory description of Yusuf al-Hallaj, providing insight into how scholars were commemorated in the Islamic tradition.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the factual content.
Notice the structure: name, lineage, teachers, students, works, praise.
2
Identify key biographical elements
List the elements: full name, lineage (al-Harawi), school (al-Shafi'i), his son as a student of al-Taftazani, his own works (commentary on al-Hawi), his notable students, and the quote from his student.
This shows the standard format of a biographical notice.
3
Understand the function of such entries
Reflect on why biographical entries like this are important: they establish chains of transmission (sanad) and scholarly authority, which is critical in Islamic sciences including kalam.
Knowing who studied under whom helps verify knowledge transmission.
4
Analyze the praise from his student
Read the student's description: 'one to whom journeys are undertaken...'. Consider what qualities were prized in a scholar (reliability, mastery, heroic stature).

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٩ - يوسف الحلاج هو يوسف الجمال الحلاج الهروي الشافعي، والد أخذ الشمس محمد الماضي ممن عن التفتازاني وغيره، وتقدم في الفضائل، وشرح الحاوي شرحاً متوسطاً وانتفع به الفضلاء كولده الشمس، ومحد بن موسى الحاجري شيخ التقي الحصني . ويصفه تلميذه التقي الحسني بقوله : ممن تشد له الرحال ويعول عليه في كشف المقال والحال زبدة الأفاضل الماهرين الماجد الهمام جمال الدنيا والدین (۲) .
9 - Yusuf al-Hallaj: He is Yusuf al-Jamal al-Hallaj al-Harawi al-Shafi'i, the father of the taken (student) Shams Muhammad al-Madi, from those who studied under al-Taftazani and others, and he excelled in virtues. His student Taqi al-Husni describes him saying: 'One to whom journeys are undertaken, relied upon in uncovering speech and state, the cream of excellent masters, the magnificent hero, the beauty of the world and religion (2).'

Commentary
This passage is a biographical entry for Yusuf al-Hallaj, identifying him by his full name, lineage (al-Harawi indicates Herat), and school (Shafi'i). It states that his son Shams Muhammad was a student of al-Taftazani and others, and that Yusuf himself excelled in virtues. He authored a medium-length commentary on al-Hawi (likely a work in fiqh or hadith). The passage lists notable students who benefited from him, including his own son Shams and Muhammad ibn Musa al-Hajari, who was the teacher of Taqi al-Husni. The biography concludes with a laudatory quote from his student Taqi al-Husni, praising Yusuf as a highly esteemed scholar.

Introduces Jalal al-Din and reproduces an ijaza from al-Taftazani.

Core Idea
An ijaza is a scholar's personal authorization to teach and transmit his works.

This lesson introduces a biographical notice about Jalal al-Din Yusuf, a student of the renowned Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani. More importantly, it presents the full text of an ijaza (teaching license) written by al-Taftazani, which sheds light on the academic relationship between master and student in the Islamic scholarly tradition.

Guided Path

Read the biographical note
Understand who Jalal al-Din is and his connection to al-Taftazani as a student.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١٠ - جلال الدين يوسف بن ركن الدين مسيح : يتفق بعض المؤرخين بأنه كان من تلامذة سعد الدين التفتازاني، ويقدمون بين ذلك إجازة كتبها التفتازاني إلى تلميذه جلال الدين بتغيير مصنفاته وقراءتها ،وإصلاحها، وهذه صورة الإجازة : أما بعد، حمداً لله والصلاة على رسول الله، فقد أجزت للمولى العالم أن الفاضل الكامل جلال الدين يوسف بن الإمام المرحوم ركن الدين مسيح يروي عني مقروءاتي ومسموعاتي ومنجزاتي عموماً ومصنفاتي خصوصاً فقد قرأ الكثير وسمع الكثير مثل شرح الكشاف، والمفتاح وغيرهما، وأن يدرسهما ويصلح ما يتفق أنه من سهو البنان أو البيان بعد التأمل والاحتياط والمراجعة والمطالعة. وهذا خط الفقير سعد الدين التفتازاني كتبه في آخر سفر حياته، والاتصال بوفاته، وهو الأواخر من محرم سنة ٧٩٢ سنة ۷۹۲ه بسمرقند
10 - Jalal al-Din Yusuf ibn Rukn al-Din Masih: Some historians agree that he was among the students of Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani, and they present in that regard an ijaza written by al-Taftazani to his student Jalal al-Din, authorizing the transmission, reading, and correction of his works. This is the text of the ijaza: 'To proceed: Praise be to God and blessings upon the Messenger of God. I have granted permission to the noble, virtuous, perfect master Jalal al-Din Yusuf ibn the late imam Rukn al-Din Masih to narrate from me my readings, hearings, and completed works generally, and my authored works particularly, for he has read much and heard much, such as Sharh al-Kashshaf and al-Miftah and others, and to teach them and correct whatever might occur of slips of the pen or expression after careful consideration, caution, review, and study. This is the writing of the poor servant Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani, written in the last journey of his life, connected to his death, in the last days of Muharram of the year 792 AH in Samarkand.'
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage is a biographical entry for Jalal al-Din Yusuf ibn Rukn al-Din Masih, identified as a student of al-Taftazani. It presents a full ijaza (teaching license) from al-Taftazani to Jalal al-Din. The ijaza begins with the basmala and praises, then states that al-Taftazani grants permission for Jalal al-Din to narrate all of his readings and authored works, specifically mentioning Sharh al-Kashshaf and al-Miftah (likely al-Miftah in balagha). The ijaza also authorizes him to teach and correct these works, with the condition of careful review. The ijaza is dated to the last days of Muharram 792 AH, in Samarkand, and notes that it was written at the end of al-Taftazani's life.

Key Terms
إجازة
شرح الكشاف
المفتاح
سعد الدين التفتازاني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An ijaza is a diploma certifying completion of a course of study.
Correction: An ijaza is a personal license from a scholar authorizing the recipient to transmit, teach, and sometimes correct the scholar's works. It is not a certificate of completion but a permission based on the scholar's trust in the student's competence.

Checkpoint
What specific works does the ijaza mention that Jalal al-Din read?
A) Sharh al-Mawaqif and al-Miftah
B) Sharh al-Kashshaf and al-Miftah
C) al-Hawi and al-Miftah
D) Sharh al-Matali' and al-Miftah

Key Takeaway
The ijaza from al-Taftazani demonstrates the personal and trust-based nature of scholarly transmission in the Islamic tradition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The ijaza was written in the last days of al-Taftazani's life. If you knew you had only days left, what knowledge would you personally entrust to a student to carry on? Who would that student be?
?
Al-Taftazani allowed Jalal al-Din to correct 'slips of the pen or expression' but only 'after careful consideration, caution, review, and study.' When you learn and teach, do you approach correction with the same humility and rigor, or do you assume you are already accurate?
?
The ijaza is a trust-based authorization, not a mere certificate. Think of someone who has taught you sincerely: have you honored their trust by transmitting what they taught you accurately and with proper attribution?

The Identity of Mirik al-Sayrani
Identifies Mirik al-Sayrani as a student of al-Taftazani and equates him with Yahya al-Sirami.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly identification of Mirik al-Sayrani and his connection to the balagha tradition through his teacher al-Taftazani and his gloss on al-Mutawwal.

Core Idea
A gloss on al-Mutawwal ties Mirik al-Sayrani to the great scholar al-Taftazani.

This lesson introduces a biographical entry from al-Sakhawi's al-Daw' al-Lami', identifying a scholar named Mirik al-Sayrani as a student of Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani and equating him with Yahya al-Sirami, author of a gloss on al-Mutawwal. Understanding such identifications is crucial for tracing chains of transmission in Islamic scholarship.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without overthinking, then read the literal translation. Let the biographical details emerge naturally.
Notice the use of the word 'لعل' (perhaps), indicating a conjecture.
2
Identify the primary source
The passage quotes from al-Daw' al-Lami' by al-Sakhawi. Note how the author uses this source to establish Mirik's scholarly lineage.
3
Examine the chain of transmission
The passage states that Mirik was a student of al-Taftazani and that Abd al-Salam al-Qalyubi studied under Mirik. This connects key scholars in the balagha tradition.
This chain is typical of Islamic scholarly networks.
4
Reflect on the identification
Consider why the author suggests Mirik al-Sayrani is Yahya al-Sirami. What evidence is cited? (Kashf al-Zunun and Hadiyat al-Arifin) Why might this identification be important?
Such identifications help clarify the authorship of works like the gloss on al-Mutawwal.

۱۱ - ميرك الصيراني أو السيرامي: ذكره صاحب الضوء اللامع في ترجمة الإمام عبد السلام بن أحمد الحسيني القليوبي حيث قال : إنه قرأ كثيراً من شروح التلخيص في المعاني، وكثيراً من الكشاف على مولاه ميرك السيرامي أحد تلامذة سعد الدين التفتازاني ويقول صاحب الضوء اللامع : لعل ميرك الصيراني هو يحيى بن يوسف المصري الحنفي المعروف بالسيرامي المتوفى سنة ۸۲۳ هـ والذي صنف حاشية في البلاغة على كتاب المطول كما ذكره صاحب كشف الظنون وصاحب كتاب هدية العارفين (١) .
11 - Mirik al-Sayrani or al-Sirami: The author of al-Daw' al-Lami' mentioned him in the biography of Imam Abd al-Salam ibn Ahmad al-Husayni al-Qalyubi, saying: 'He read many of the commentaries on al-Talkhis in al-ma'ani, and much of al-Kashshaf under his master Mirik al-Sirami, one of the students of Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani.' The Ijazah of al-Taftazani: A Model of Knowledge Transmission
Al-Taftazani grants a formal license to a student authorizing transmission of his works.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what an ijazah is and how al-Taftazani's ijazah to his student illustrates the traditional system of authorizing knowledge transmission.

Core Idea
An ijazah is a personal license to transmit knowledge, linking teacher and student across generations.

In this passage, we see a classical ijazah from the renowned scholar Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani to his student Jalal al-Din Yusuf. It is both a personal document and a window into the traditional system of knowledge transmission. As you read, notice the specific language of authorization and the texts mentioned.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall meaning.
Pay attention to the formal opening and the specific terms.
2
Identify the key terms
Look for terms like إجازة, مقروءات, مسموعات, etc. Understand what each refers to in the context of traditional education.
These terms are the building blocks of the ijazah system.
3
Understand the scope of permission
Notice what exactly al-Taftazani authorizes: his readings (maqru'at), heard materials (masmu'at), authored works (munjazat), and specifically his compositions (musannafat).
The student is also tasked with correcting errors.
4
Reflect on historical context
Consider why the ijazah was written near the end of al-Taftazani's life and in Samarqand. How does this document illustrate the personal nature of knowledge transmission?
The date 792 AH is key; note possible OCR error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما بعد، حمداً لله والصلاة على رسول الله، فقد أجزت للمولى العالم أن الفاضل الكامل جلال الدين يوسف بن الإمام المرحوم ركن الدين مسيح يروي عني مقروءاتي ومسموعاتي ومنجزاتي عموماً ومصنفاتي خصوصاً فقد قرأ الكثير وسمع الكثير مثل شرح الكشاف، والمفتاح وغيرهما، وأن يدرسهما ويصلح ما يتفق أنه من سهو البنان أو البيان بعد التأمل والاحتياط والمراجعة والمطالعة. وهذا خط الفقير سعد الدين التفتازاني كتبه في آخر سفر حياته، والاتصال بوفاته، وهو الأواخر من محرم سنة ٥٧٩٢ سنة ۷۹۲ه بسمرقند
To proceed: praise be to God and prayers upon the Messenger of God. I have granted permission to the master, the learned, the virtuous and perfect Jalal al-Din Yusuf, son of the late Imam Rukn al-Din Masih, to narrate from me what I have read, heard, and authored, generally, and my compositions specifically. For he has read much and heard much, such as the commentary on al-Kashshaf, al-Miftah, and others, and that he study them and correct whatever occurs of slips of the pen or expression after reflection, caution, revision, and study. This is the handwriting of the poor one Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani, written at the end of his life's journey and at the point of his death, in the last days of Muharram of the year 792 AH in Samarqand.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage is an ijazah (academic license) from Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani to his student Jalal al-Din Yusuf. It begins with the traditional formula 'Amma ba'd' followed by praise. Al-Taftazani grants permission for the student to transmit his oral readings, heard materials, and authored works. The student had studied key texts like the commentary on al-Zamakhshari's al-Kashshaf and al-Sakkaki's al-Miftah. The ijazah also commissions the student to correct any errors. The text concludes with al-Taftazani's attestation that this is his handwriting, written shortly before his death in 792 AH in Samarqand. The date contains a likely OCR error; the correct year is 792 AH.

Key Terms
إجازة
مقروءات
مسموعات
منجزات
مصنفات
سهو البنان
سهو البيان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This ijazah is a general teaching license for anyone.
Correction: The ijazah is a personal authorization for a specific student, Jalal al-Din Yusuf, and covers only the transmission of al-Taftazani's own materials.

Checkpoint
What does al-Taftazani authorize Jalal al-Din Yusuf to do?
A) Teach in Samarqand
B) Transmit al-Taftazani's readings, hearings, and works
C) Write a commentary on al-Kashshaf
D) Correct other students' mistakes

Key Takeaway
The ijazah is a personal, not institutional, license that ensures authentic transmission of knowledge from teacher to student.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn something about your faith, do you seek out a living teacher from whom you can receive it personally, or do you rely only on books and videos? What would it take for you to pursue an actual teacher-student relationship in Islamic knowledge?
?
Al-Taftazani's permission includes the responsibility to correct errors. Is there a belief or practice you hold that you have never had verified by a qualified scholar? Could you be perpetuating a mistake? What step will you take today to check your understanding?
?
This ijazah was written at the end of al-Taftazani's life. If you were to write your own 'ijazah' of the knowledge you want to pass on, what would it include? Are you actively preparing a legacy of authentic knowledge, or are you just consuming?

Core Idea
A scholar's identity is often uncovered through teacher-student chains and textual affiliations.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid introduces Mirik al-Sayrani, a student of the famous Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani. Through references from al-Sakhawi's al-Daw' al-Lami', the author pieces together his identity, highlighting the importance of intellectual lineage in Islamic scholarship. ١١ - ميرك الصيراني أو السيرامي: ذكره صاحب الضوء اللامع في ترجمة الإمام عبد السلام بن أحمد الحسيني القليوبي حيث قال : إنه قرأ كثيراً من شروح التلخيص في المعاني، وكثيراً من الكشاف على مولاه ميرك السيرامي أحد تلامذة سعد الدين التفتازاني ويقول صاحب الضوء اللامع : لعل ميرك الصيراني هو يحيى بن يوسف المصري الحنفي المعروف بالسيرامي المتوفى سنة ۸۲۳ هـ والذي صنف حاشية في البلاغة على كتاب المطول كما ذكره صاحب كشف الظنون وصاحب كتاب هدية العارفين (١) .
11 - Mirik al-Sayrani or al-Sirami: The author of al-Daw' al-Lami' mentioned him in the biography of Imam 'Abd al-Salam ibn Ahmad al-Husayni al-Qalyubi, where he said: He read many of the commentaries on al-Talkhis in al-Ma'ani, and much of al-Kashshaf under his master Mirik al-Sirami, one of the students of Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani. And the author of al-Daw' al-Lami' says: Perhaps Mirik al-Sayrani is Yahya ibn Yusuf al-Misri al-Hanafi known as al-Sirami, who died in 823 AH and composed a gloss on rhetoric on the book al-Mutawwal, as mentioned by the author of Kashf al-Zunun and the author of Hadiyyat al-'Arifin (1).

Commentary
This passage is a biographical entry for a scholar named Mirik al-Sayrani or al-Sirami. The author cites al-Sakhawi's al-Daw' al-Lami' to show that this scholar was a teacher of 'Abd al-Salam al-Qalyubi, and himself a student of al-Taftazani. The text then speculates on his full identity, suggesting he may be Yahya ibn Yusuf al-Hanafi, known for a gloss on al-Mutawwal, a work on rhetoric. The footnote reference (1) directs the reader to Kashf al-Zunun and Hadiyyat al-'Arifin for confirmation. The passage highlights the transmission of knowledge through the Taftazani chain.

Misconception: The name 'Mirik' is thought to be a proper given name.
Correction: In this context, 'Mirik' is a Persian title meaning 'master' or 'sayyid', used as an honorific. The scholar's actual name is likely Yahya ibn Yusuf.

Biographical entry citing al-Sakhawi's two references.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly lineage and breadth of subjects transmitted by Lutfallah al-Samarqandi, a student of al-Taftazani.

Core Idea
Lutfallah al-Samarqandi links al-Taftazani to the next generation of scholars.

This lesson introduces a brief but informative biographical entry about Lutfallah al-Samarqandi, as recorded by Imam al-Sakhawi. The passage reveals his role as a transmitter of knowledge from the renowned Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani to students like Ibrahim al-Shihawi. Through this entry, we glimpse how classical scholars documented chains of learning across multiple disciplines.

These subjects cover both rational sciences (logic, rhetoric) and religious principles.

Commentary
This passage is a biographical entry for Lutfallah al-Samarqandi. The author quotes al-Sakhawi's al-Daw' al-Lami' twice. Second, in al-Sakhawi's own separate entry, he simply lists Lutfallah as one of al-Taftazani's students. The passage emphasizes the breadth of subjects transmitted by Lutfallah, including both the rational and transmitted sciences.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
Some scholars are known only through the references of their students.

In this brief entry, the author documents a scholar about whom virtually nothing is known beyond his connection to two major figures: al-Taftazani and al-Jami. This lesson explores how such fragmentary records still contribute to our understanding of scholarly networks.

This is a common method in Islamic bio-bibliographical literature.

Not all figures in the scholarly chain are well-documented.

Shams al-Din al-Fanari and the Spread of Knowledge
Author details al-Fanari's role in popularizing al-Taftazani's works.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Shams al-Din al-Fanari facilitated the copying and spread of al-Taftazani's works by adjusting the academic calendar.

Core Idea
A simple schedule change helped preserve and propagate a scholar's legacy.

This lesson introduces Shams al-Din al-Fanari, a Hanafi judge and student of al-Taftazani. His story shows how practical decisions—like adding a holiday for book copying—can have a lasting impact on knowledge transmission. Read on to see how one scholar's initiative made rare texts accessible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Pay attention to names, dates, and the problem described.
The passage begins with a standard biographical formula before narrating the key event.
2
Identify al-Fanari's contribution
Find the sentence that states what al-Fanari did. Ask yourself: what problem did he solve, and how did his action benefit students?
The problem: al-Taftazani's books were unavailable for purchase and needed to be copied. The existing holidays were insufficient.
3
Reflect on the impact
Consider why this act was considered 'essential' for spreading al-Taftazani's works. How does this story illustrate the importance of supporting scholarship?
This connects to broader themes in Islamic educational history.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١٤ - شمس الدين الفنري : هو محمد بن حمزة العلامة قاضي القضاة شمس الدين أبو عبد الله الفنري الرومي الحنفي. كان عارفاً بالعربية والمعاني، يحدد المؤرخون تاريخ ولادته بعام ٧٥١ هـ وجاء في ترجمة حفيده محمد بن عمر بن محمد بن حمزة أن جده هذا كان من بلاد ما وراء النهر، من تلامذة سعد الدين التفتازاني . ويرى بعض المحققين والراصدين للفكر الإنساني أن شمس الدين كان سبباً جوهرياً في إظهار كتب العلامة التفتازاني، إذ إنها انتشرت ورغب الطلبة في قراءتها ولم تكن موجودة بالشراء، لعدم انتشار نسخها، فاحتاجوا إلى كتابتها، ولكن عطلتهم الأسبوعية وهي يوم الجمعة والثلاثاء لم تكن وقتاً كافياً لكتابة هذه الكتب فأضاف شمس الدين يوم الاثنين إلى العطلة ليتمكن الطلبة من التزود بكتب التفتازاني
14 - Shams al-Din al-Fanari: He is Muhammad ibn Hamza, the highly learned chief judge Shams al-Din Abu 'Abd Allah al-Fanari al-Rumi al-Hanafi. He was knowledgeable in Arabic and rhetoric. Historians set his birth year at 751 AH. It came in the biography of his grandson Muhammad ibn 'Umar ibn Muhammad ibn Hamza that this grandfather was from beyond the river, among the students of Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani. Some researchers and observers of human thought believe that Shams al-Din was an essential cause in bringing out the books of the eminent al-Taftazani, for they spread and students desired to read them but they were not available for purchase due to the scarcity of copies, so they needed to write them. But their weekly holidays—Friday and Tuesday—were not sufficient time for writing these books, so Shams al-Din added Monday to the holiday to enable the students to supply themselves with al-Taftazani's books.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is a biographical entry for Shams al-Din al-Fanari, a Hanafi judge and scholar. It provides his full name, title, and birth year (751 AH). It then cites his grandson's biography to confirm that al-Fanari was from Transoxiana and a student of al-Taftazani. The core anecdote explains al-Fanari's role in spreading al-Taftazani's works: when students wanted to copy the books but found the existing weekly holidays (Friday and Tuesday) insufficient, al-Fanari added Monday as an extra holiday, allowing students time to transcribe the texts. This act is portrayed as crucial for the dissemination of al-Taftazani's scholarship.

Key Terms

قاضي القضاة
ما وراء النهر

انتشار الكتب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Shams al-Din al-Fanari wrote commentaries or edited al-Taftazani's works.
Correction: Al-Fanari did not edit or comment on al-Taftazani's books; he facilitated their copying by adding a holiday for transcription.

Key Takeaway
Shams al-Din al-Fanari's addition of Monday as a holiday enabled students to copy al-Taftazani's rare books, playing a vital role in their dissemination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Biographical entry quoting al-Sakhawi on al-Athir al-Baghdadi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly lineage connecting al-Athir al-Baghdadi to al-Taftazani and the texts studied in the traditional curriculum.

Core Idea
Al-Athir al-Baghdadi bridges two generations of Islamic scholarship.

This lesson introduces a lesser-known scholar, al-Athir al-Baghdadi, who was a direct student of the famous Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani. Through his biography, we glimpse the chain of transmission and core texts in grammar and legal theory. Though the entry is brief and incomplete, it reveals how knowledge was passed down.

This lesson introduces three scholars connected to al-Taftazani, highlighting their roles in preserving and transmitting his legacy. The third entry provides details on al-Athir al-Baghdadi's studies and the silence of later biographers.

قال :
۱۳ - شهاب الدین محمد
ذكره صاحب الفوائد البهية في ترجمة نور الدين عبد الرحمن الجامي حيث إنه حضر دروس مولانا شهاب الدين محمد تلميذ التفتازاني . ولم نعثر على شيء آخر يوضح شخصيته بالرغم من كثرة تفتيشنا وبحثنا في كتب التراجم والأعلام» (۱)
١٤ - شمس الدين الفنري : هو محمد بن حمزة العلامة قاضي القضاة شمس الدين أبو عبد الله الفنري الرومي الحنفي.
كان عارفاً بالعربية والمعاني، يحدد المؤرخون تاريخ ولادته بعام ٧٥١ هـ وجاء في ترجمة حفيده محمد بن عمر بن محمد بن حمزة أن جده هذا كان من بلاد ما وراء النهر، من تلامذة سعد الدين التفتازاني .
ويرى بعض المحققين والراصدين للفكر الإنساني أن شمس الدين كان سبباً جوهرياً في إظهار كتب العلامة التفتازاني، إذ إنها انتشرت ورغب الطلبة في قراءتها ولم تكن موجودة بالشراء، لعدم انتشار نسخها، فاحتاجوا إلى كتابتها، ولكن عطلتهم الأسبوعية وهي يوم الجمعة والثلاثاء لم تكن وقتاً كافياً لكتابة هذه الكتب فأضاف شمس الدين يوم الاثنين إلى العطلة ليتمكن الطلبة من التزود بكتب التفتازاني ١٥ - الأثير البغدادي : يقول صاحب الضوء اللامع في ترجمته: هو جبريل بن صالح الأثير البغدادي.
وممن
(۲)
أحمد
تتلمذ على سعد الدين التفتازاني، وقد أخذ عنه محمود بن العيني وقرأ عليه المفصل في النحو، والتوضيح مع متنه التنقيح . ولكن متى كان
(۱) راجع الفوائد البهية للإمام اللكنوي ص ٣٤ . (۲) راجع الشقائق النعمانية ج ۲ ص ۳ وج ۱ ص ۹۰ .

مولده، وفي أي عام كانت وفاته؟ ، يصمت الإمام السخاوي عن ذلك، فلا يذكره لامن قريب ولا من بعيد - وكأنه - أراد لمن يترجم لهم أن يكونوا من أعيان القرن التاسع وكفى
(۱)
١٦ - سعد الدين لر :
وهذا لم نعثر له على ترجمة، ولم يذكر في تاريخ التفتازاني، ولكن يذكره السخاوي في ترجمة أبي الحسن علي الكرماني على أنه من شيوخه الفضلاء
فقال :
وقال :
ومن شيوخه سعد الدين لر الذي كان من طلبة التفتازاني )
۱۷ - قره داود
(۲)
ذكره صاحب كتاب كشف الظنون عند ذكره للسيد الشريف الجرجاني،
(۳)
له حاشية على شرح الشمسية لقطب الدين التحتاني، وعلى هذه الحاشية حواشي كثيرة منها : حاشية للمولى قره دواد من تلامذة سعد الدين التفتازاني ولم يذكره صاحب النجوم الزاهرة، ولا صاحب الضوء اللامع، وبذلك لا يمكن تحديد تاريخ ولادته أو وفاته، وهذه الحيرة التي يعيش فيها الباحث الذي يتعامل مع كتب التراث لا يقدرها حق قدرها إلا من عاش مثل هذه المعاناة .
هو
۱۸ - فتح الله الشرواني تتفق الشقائق النعمانية، ومفتاح السعادة في أنه فتح الله بن عبد الله الشرواني الرومي الحنفي.
أخذ
عن العلامة التفتازاني ، والسيد الشريف الجرجاني، واستفاد منهما العلوم العقلية والشرعية. ومن تصانيفه شرح كتاب إرشاد الهادي في النحو
(۱) راجع الضوء اللامع ج ۱۰ ص ۱۳۱ .
(۲) راجع الضوء اللامع ج ٦٠ ص ٥٧ . (۳) راجع كتاب كشف الظنون ج ٢ ص ١٠٦٣ .
He said:
13 - Shihab al-Din Muhammad
The author of al-Fawa'id al-Bahiyyah mentioned him in the biography of Nur al-Din 'Abd al-Rahman al-Jami, as he attended the lessons of our master Shihab al-Din Muhammad, a student of al-Taftazani. And we did not find anything else clarifying his personality despite our extensive searching and research in biographical works and reference books. (1)
14 - Shams al-Din al-Fanari: He is Muhammad ibn Hamzah, the scholar, qadi of qadis, Shams al-Din Abu 'Abd Allah al-Fanari al-Rumi al-Hanafi.
He was knowledgeable in Arabic and semantics. Historians determine his birth date as 751 AH. And it comes in the biography of his grandson Muhammad ibn 'Umar ibn Muhammad ibn Hamzah that this grandfather was from Transoxiana, from the students of Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani.
And some researchers and observers of human thought hold that Shams al-Din was a crucial reason for the emergence of the books of the 'Allamah al-Taftazani, for they spread and students desired to read them, and they were not available for purchase due to the lack of circulation of their copies, so they needed to copy them. But their weekly holidays, which were Friday and Tuesday, were not sufficient time to write these books, so Shams al-Din added Monday to the holiday so that students could supply themselves with al-Taftazani's books. 15 - al-Athir al-Baghdadi: The author of al-Daw' al-Lami' says in his biography: He is Jibril ibn Salih al-Athir al-Baghdadi.
And among those who
(2)
Ahmad
studied under Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani, and Mahmud ibn al-'Ayni took from him and read to him al-Mufassal in grammar, and al-Tawdih along with its matn al-Tanqih. But when was
(1) Refer to al-Fawa'id al-Bahiyyah by al-Imam al-Laknawi, p. 34. (2) Refer to al-Shaqa'iq al-Nu'maniyyah, vol. 2, p. 3 and vol. 1, p. 90.

his birth, and in which year was his death? Imam al-Sakhawi remains silent about that, not mentioning it at all - as if he intended that those for whom he writes biographies should be from the notables of the ninth century, and that is sufficient.
(1)
16 - Sa'd al-Din Lar:
And we did not find a biography for him, and he is not mentioned in the history of al-Taftazani, but al-Sakhawi mentions him in the biography of Abu al-Hasan 'Ali al-Kirmani as one of his distinguished teachers.

And he said:
And among his teachers is Sa'd al-Din Lar, who was from the students of al-Taftazani.)
17 - Qara Dawud
(2)
The author of Kashf al-Zunun mentioned him when mentioning al-Sayyid al-Sharif al-Jurjani,
(3)
He has a gloss on Sharh al-Shamsiyyah by Qutb al-Din al-Tahtani, and on this gloss there are many glosses, including: a gloss by al-Mulla Qara Dawud, from the students of Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani. And he was not mentioned by the author of al-Nujum al-Zahirah, nor by the author of al-Daw' al-Lami', and thus it is not possible to determine his birth or death date. And this confusion that the researcher who deals with heritage books experiences is not truly appreciated except by those who have suffered such hardship.
He is
18 - Fath Allah al-Shirwani: al-Shaqa'iq al-Nu'maniyyah and Miftah al-Sa'adah agree that he is Fath Allah ibn 'Abd Allah al-Shirwani al-Rumi al-Hanafi.
He took from
al-'Allamah al-Taftazani and al-Sayyid al-Sharif al-Jurjani, and benefited from them in the intellectual and legal sciences. Among his works is a commentary on the book Irshad al-Hadi in grammar.
(1) Refer to al-Daw' al-Lami', vol. 10, p. 131.
(2) Refer to al-Daw' al-Lami', vol. 6, p. 57. (3) Refer to Kashf al-Zunun, vol. 2, p. 1063.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is a series of six biographical entries from the introduction of Sharh al-Maqasid, listing scholars connected to al-Taftazani. The author (the editor or commentator) provides what is known about each figure, often citing sources. The passage proceeds in numerical order:

1. Entry 13 (Shihab al-Din Muhammad): The author notes that this person is mentioned by al-Laknawi in his biography of al-Jami as having attended lessons of Shihab al-Din Muhammad, a student of al-Taftazani. Despite extensive research, no further biographical details are found.

2. Entry 14 (Shams al-Din al-Fanari): Full name given as Muhammad ibn Hamzah, a Hanafi judge. He was knowledgeable in Arabic and semantics; born 751 AH. His grandson's biography indicates he was from Transoxiana and a student of al-Taftazani. The author adds that some researchers credit al-Fanari with a crucial role in promoting al-Taftazani's works: he added Monday to the weekly holidays (Friday and Tuesday) to allow students to copy the books, which were not available for purchase.

3. Entry 15 (al-Athir al-Baghdadi): Quoting al-Sakhawi's al-Daw' al-Lami', he is identified as Jibril ibn Salih. He studied under al-Taftazani, and Mahmud ibn al-'Ayni read to him works like al-Mufassal and al-Tawdih with al-Tanqih. Al-Sakhawi remains silent about his birth and death, possibly because he only meant to include notables of the ninth century.

4. Entry 16 (Sa'd al-Din Lar): No biography found; not mentioned in al-Taftazani's history. However, al-Sakhawi in his biography of al-Kirmani lists him as a distinguished teacher who was a student of al-Taftazani.

5. Entry 17 (Qara Dawud): Mentioned by Katib Çelebi in Kashf al-Zunun concerning al-Jurjani. He wrote a gloss on Qutb al-Din al-Tahtani's Sharh al-Shamsiyyah, which itself has many glosses, including one by Qara Dawud. Neither al-Nujum al-Zahirah nor al-Daw' al-Lami' mention him, so his dates are unknown. The author reflects on the difficulty of such research.

6. Entry 18 (Fath Allah al-Shirwani): Agreed upon as Fath Allah ibn 'Abd Allah, a Hanafi. He studied under both al-Taftazani and al-Jurjani, benefiting in intellectual and legal sciences. Among his works is a commentary on Irshad al-Hadi in grammar. The footnotes cite sources for each entry.

Key Terms

شمس الدين الفنري
الأثير البغدادي
تلميذ
حاشية

Checkpoint
What did Shams al-Din al-Fanari add to the weekly holiday to help students copy books?
A) Tuesday
B) Monday
C) Friday
D) Thursday

Key Takeaway
Al-Taftazani's students varied in fame, but their collective efforts helped preserve his works.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson highlights a student (Shams al-Din) who sacrificed an extra day to help others access knowledge. What is one concrete sacrifice (time, comfort, or resources) you are making today to ensure that beneficial knowledge reaches others? Or are you only consuming without contributing? Name one action you will take this week.
?
These students are largely forgotten by history, yet they played an essential role. If you knew that your efforts in seeking and spreading knowledge would be completely forgotten within a generation, would you still invest your energy as sincerely as you do now? What would change in your approach?
?
Al-Sakhawi chose not to record al-Athir's birth and death details because he only included 'notables of the ninth century'. Are you more concerned with being recognized as a notable or with being a hidden servant of knowledge for Allah's sake? Which motivation drives your study of 'Aqeedah today?
